   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Nuclear fusion
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      This article is about the science behind nuclear fusion. For its use in producing energy, see
      <a href="/wiki/Fusion_power" title="Fusion power">
       fusion power
      </a>
      .
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="441" data-file-width="671" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg/400px-Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg/600px-Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg/800px-Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg.png 2x" width="400"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_binding_energy" title="Nuclear binding energy">
         nuclear binding energy
        </a>
        curve. The formation of nuclei with masses up to
        <a href="/wiki/Iron-56" title="Iron-56">
         Iron-56
        </a>
        releases energy, while forming those that are heavier requires energy input. This is because the nuclei below Iron-56 have high binding energies, while the heavier ones have lower binding energies, as illustrated above.
       </div>
      </div>

      <tr>
       <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
        <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
         <ul>
          <li class="nv-view">
           <a href="/wiki/Template:Nuclear_physics" title="Template:Nuclear physics">
            <abbr title="View this template">
             v
            </abbr>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="nv-talk">
           <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Nuclear_physics" title="Template talk:Nuclear physics">
            <abbr title="Discuss this template">
             t
            </abbr>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="nv-edit">
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Nuclear_physics&amp;action=edit">
            <abbr title="Edit this template">
             e
            </abbr>
           </a>
          </li>
         </ul>
        </div>

      </tr>
     </table>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3860" data-file-width="4044" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg/250px-The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg/375px-The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg/500px-The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
         Sun
        </a>
        is a
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Main-sequence_star" title="Main-sequence star">
         main-sequence star
        </a>
        , and thus generates its
        <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">
         energy
        </a>
        by nuclear fusion of
        <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
         hydrogen
        </a>
        nuclei into
        <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">
         helium
        </a>
        . In its core, the Sun fuses 620 million
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_ton" title="Metric ton">
         metric tons
        </a>
        of hydrogen each second.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_physics" title="Nuclear physics">
       nuclear physics
      </a>
      ,
      <b>
       nuclear fusion
      </b>
      is a
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">
       nuclear reaction
      </a>
      in which two or more
      <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">
       atomic nuclei
      </a>
      come close enough to form one or more different atomic nuclei and subatomic particles (neutrons and/or protons). The difference in mass between the products and reactants is manifested as the release of large amounts of energy. This difference in mass arises due to the difference in atomic "
      <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">
       binding energy
      </a>
      " between the atomic nuclei before and after the reaction. Fusion is the process that powers active or "
      <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">
       main sequence
      </a>
      "
      <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">
       stars
      </a>
      , or other
      <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude" title="Absolute magnitude">
       high magnitude
      </a>
      stars.
     </p>
     <p>
      The fusion process that produces a nucleus lighter than
      <a href="/wiki/Iron-56" title="Iron-56">
       iron-56
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Nickel-62" title="Nickel-62">
       nickel-62
      </a>
      will generally yield a net energy release. These elements have the smallest mass per nucleon and the largest
      <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">
       binding energy
      </a>
      per
      <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">
       nucleon
      </a>
      , respectively. Fusion of light elements toward these releases energy (an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exothermic" title="Exothermic">
       exothermic
      </a>
      process), while a fusion producing nuclei heavier than these elements, will result in energy retained by the resulting nucleons, and the resulting reaction is
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endothermic" title="Endothermic">
       endothermic
      </a>
      . The opposite is true for the reverse process,
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">
       nuclear fission
      </a>
      . This means that the lighter elements, such as hydrogen and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helium_fusion" title="Helium fusion">
       helium
      </a>
      , are in general more fusable; while the heavier elements, such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uranium_235" title="Uranium 235">
       uranium
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plutonium_239" title="Plutonium 239">
       plutonium
      </a>
      , are more fissionable. The extreme
      <a href="/wiki/Astrophysics" title="Astrophysics">
       astrophysical
      </a>
      event of a
      <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">
       supernova
      </a>
      can produce enough energy to fuse nuclei into elements heavier than iron.
     </p>
     <p>
      Following the discovery of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_tunneling" title="Quantum tunneling">
       quantum tunneling
      </a>
      by physicist
      <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hund" title="Friedrich Hund">
       Friedrich Hund
      </a>
      , in 1929
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_d%27Escourt_Atkinson" title="Robert d'Escourt Atkinson">
       Robert Atkinson
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Houtermans" title="Fritz Houtermans">
       Fritz Houtermans
      </a>
      used the measured masses of light elements to predict that large amounts of energy could be released by fusing small nuclei. Building upon the
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">
       nuclear transmutation
      </a>
      experiments by
      <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">
       Ernest Rutherford
      </a>
      , carried out several years earlier, the laboratory fusion of
      <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_hydrogen" title="Isotopes of hydrogen">
       hydrogen isotopes
      </a>
      was first accomplished by
      <a href="/wiki/Mark_Oliphant" title="Mark Oliphant">
       Mark Oliphant
      </a>
      in 1932. During the remainder of that decade the steps of the main cycle of nuclear fusion in stars were worked out by
      <a href="/wiki/Hans_Bethe" title="Hans Bethe">
       Hans Bethe
      </a>
      . Research into fusion for military purposes began in the early 1940s as part of the
      <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">
       Manhattan Project
      </a>
      . Fusion was accomplished in 1951 with the
      <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_Item" title="Greenhouse Item">
       Greenhouse Item
      </a>
      nuclear test. Nuclear fusion on a large scale in an explosion was first carried out on November 1, 1952, in the
      <a href="/wiki/Ivy_Mike" title="Ivy Mike">
       Ivy Mike
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bomb" title="Hydrogen bomb">
       hydrogen bomb
      </a>
      test.
     </p>
     <p>
      Research into developing controlled
      <a href="/wiki/Thermonuclear_fusion" title="Thermonuclear fusion">
       thermonuclear fusion
      </a>
      for civil purposes also began in earnest in the 1950s, and it continues to this day.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Process">
       Process
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Process">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="276" data-file-width="248" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg/220px-Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg/330px-Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg/440px-Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Δ
        <i>
         mc
        </i>
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
        |, where
        <i>
         Δ
        </i>
        m is the decrease in the total rest mass of particles.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shultis_1-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Shultis-1">
          [1]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The origin of the energy released in fusion of light elements is due to interplay of two opposing forces, the
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_force" title="Nuclear force">
       nuclear force
      </a>
      which combines together protons and neutrons, and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coulomb_force" title="Coulomb force">
       Coulomb force
      </a>
      , which causes protons to repel each other. The protons are positively charged and repel each other but they nonetheless stick together, demonstrating the existence of another force referred to as nuclear attraction. This force, called the strong nuclear force, overcomes electric repulsion at very close range. The effect of this force is not observed outside the nucleus, hence the force is called a short-range force. The same force also pulls the nucleons (neutrons and protons) together allowing ordinary matter to exist.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Light nuclei (or nuclei smaller than iron and nickel), are sufficiently small and proton-poor allowing the nuclear force to overcome the repulsive Coulomb force. This is because the nucleus is sufficiently small that all nucleons feel the short-range attractive force at least as strongly as they feel the infinite-range Coulomb repulsion. Building up these nuclei from lighter nuclei by
      <b>
       fusion
      </b>
      thus releases the extra energy from the net attraction of these particles.
      <a href="/wiki/Iron_peak" title="Iron peak">
       For larger nuclei
      </a>
      , however, no energy is released, since the nuclear force is short-range and cannot continue to act across still larger atomic nuclei. Thus, energy is no longer released when such nuclei are made by fusion; instead, energy is required as input to such processes.
     </p>
     <p>
      Fusion reactions create the light elements that power the
      <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">
       stars
      </a>
      and produce virtually all elements in a process called
      <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">
       nucleosynthesis
      </a>
      . The fusion of lighter elements in stars releases energy and the mass that always accompanies it. For example, in the fusion of two hydrogen nuclei to form helium, 0.7% of the mass is carried away from the system in the form of kinetic energy of an
      <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">
       alpha particle
      </a>
      or other forms of energy, such as electromagnetic radiation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bulletin1950_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-bulletin1950-3">
        [3]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Research into controlled fusion, with the aim of producing fusion power for the production of electricity, has been conducted for over 60 years. It has been accompanied by extreme scientific and technological difficulties, but has resulted in progress. At present, controlled fusion reactions have been unable to produce break-even (self-sustaining) controlled fusion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Workable designs for a reactor that theoretically will deliver ten times more fusion energy than the amount needed to heat plasma to the required temperatures are in development (see
      <a href="/wiki/ITER" title="ITER">
       ITER
      </a>
      ). The ITER facility is expected to finish its construction phase in 2019. It will start commissioning the reactor that same year and initiate plasma experiments in 2020, but is not expected to begin full deuterium-tritium fusion until 2027.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      It takes considerable energy to force nuclei to fuse, even those of the lightest element,
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
       hydrogen
      </a>
      . This is because all nuclei have a positive charge due to their protons, and as like charges repel, nuclei strongly resist being pushed close together. Accelerated to high speeds, they can overcome this electrostatic repulsion and be forced close enough such that the attractive
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_force" title="Nuclear force">
       nuclear force
      </a>
      is stronger than the repulsive force. As the strong force grows very rapidly once beyond that critical distance, the fusing nucleons "fall" into one another and result is fusion and net energy produced. The fusion of lighter nuclei, which creates a heavier nucleus and often a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_neutron" title="Free neutron">
       free neutron
      </a>
      or proton, generally releases more energy than it takes to force the nuclei together; this is an
      <a href="/wiki/Exothermic_reaction" title="Exothermic reaction">
       exothermic process
      </a>
      that can produce self-sustaining reactions. The US
      <a href="/wiki/National_Ignition_Facility" title="National Ignition Facility">
       National Ignition Facility
      </a>
      , which uses laser-driven
      <a href="/wiki/Inertial_confinement_fusion" title="Inertial confinement fusion">
       inertial confinement fusion
      </a>
      , was designed with a goal of break-even fusion.
     </p>
     <p>
      The first large-scale laser target experiments were performed in June 2009 and ignition experiments began in early 2011.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-programsNIF_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-programsNIF-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Energy
      <span id="energy">
      </span>
      released in most
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">
       nuclear reactions
      </a>
      are much larger than in
      <a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">
       chemical reactions
      </a>
      , because the
      <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">
       binding energy
      </a>
      that holds a nucleus together is far greater than the energy that holds
      <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">
       electrons
      </a>
      to a nucleus. For example, the
      <a href="/wiki/Ionization_energy" title="Ionization energy">
       ionization energy
      </a>
      gained by adding an electron to a hydrogen nucleus is
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        6982217896002232000♠
       </span>
       13.6
       <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">
        eV
       </a>
      </span>
      —less than one-millionth of the
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        6988281983061712000♠
       </span>
       17.6
       <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">
        MeV
       </a>
      </span>
      released in the
      <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">
       deuterium
      </a>
      –
      <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">
       tritium
      </a>
      (D–T) reaction shown in the diagram to the right. The complete conversion of one
      <a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">
       gram
      </a>
      of matter would release 9×10
      <sup>
       13
      </sup>
      joules of energy. Fusion reactions have an
      <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">
       energy density
      </a>
      many times greater than
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">
       nuclear fission
      </a>
      ; the reactions produce far greater energy per unit of mass even though
      <i>
       individual
      </i>
      fission reactions are generally much more energetic than
      <i>
       individual
      </i>
      fusion ones, which are themselves millions of times more energetic than chemical reactions. Only
      <a href="/wiki/Direct_energy_conversion" title="Direct energy conversion">
       direct conversion
      </a>
      of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass-energy_equivalence" title="Mass-energy equivalence">
       mass into energy
      </a>
      , such as that caused by the
      <a href="/wiki/Annihilation" title="Annihilation">
       annihilatory
      </a>
      collision of
      <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">
       matter
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">
       antimatter
      </a>
      , is more energetic per unit of mass than nuclear fusion.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_fusion_in_stars">
       Nuclear fusion in stars
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Nuclear fusion in stars">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FusionintheSun.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="966" data-file-width="678" height="356" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/FusionintheSun.svg/250px-FusionintheSun.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/FusionintheSun.svg/375px-FusionintheSun.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/FusionintheSun.svg/500px-FusionintheSun.svg.png 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FusionintheSun.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton-proton_chain" title="Proton-proton chain">
         proton-proton chain
        </a>
        dominates in stars the size of the Sun or smaller.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CNO_Cycle.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1000" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/CNO_Cycle.svg/250px-CNO_Cycle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/CNO_Cycle.svg/375px-CNO_Cycle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/CNO_Cycle.svg/500px-CNO_Cycle.svg.png 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CNO_Cycle.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/CNO_cycle" title="CNO cycle">
         CNO cycle
        </a>
        dominates in stars heavier than the Sun.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The most important fusion process in nature is the one that powers
      <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">
       stars
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">
       stellar nucleosynthesis
      </a>
      . In the 20th century, it was realized that the energy released from nuclear fusion reactions accounted for the longevity of the
      <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
       Sun
      </a>
      and other stars as a source of heat and light. The fusion of nuclei in a star, starting from its initial hydrogen and helium abundance, provides that energy and synthesizes new nuclei as a byproduct of the fusion process. The prime energy producer in the Sun is the fusion of hydrogen to form helium, which occurs at a solar-core temperature of 14 million kelvin. The net result is the fusion of four
      <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">
       protons
      </a>
      into one
      <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">
       alpha particle
      </a>
      , with the release of two
      <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">
       positrons
      </a>
      , two
      <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">
       neutrinos
      </a>
      (which changes two of the protons into neutrons), and energy. Different reaction chains are involved, depending on the mass of the star. For stars the size of the sun or smaller, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton-proton_chain" title="Proton-proton chain">
       proton-proton chain
      </a>
      dominates. In heavier stars, the
      <a href="/wiki/CNO_cycle" title="CNO cycle">
       CNO cycle
      </a>
      is more important.
     </p>
     <p>
      As a star uses up a substantial fraction of its hydrogen, it begins to synthesize heavier elements. However the heaviest elements are synthesized by fusion that occurs as a more massive star undergoes a violent
      <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">
       supernova
      </a>
      at the end of its life, a process known as
      <a href="/wiki/Supernova_nucleosynthesis" title="Supernova nucleosynthesis">
       supernova nucleosynthesis
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Requirements">
       Requirements
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Requirements">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Details and supporting references on the material in this section can be found in textbooks on nuclear physics or nuclear fusion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A substantial energy barrier of electrostatic forces must be overcome before fusion can occur. At large distances, two naked nuclei repel one another because of the repulsive
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrostatic_force" title="Electrostatic force">
       electrostatic force
      </a>
      between their
      <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">
       positively charged
      </a>
      protons. If two nuclei can be brought close enough together, however, the electrostatic repulsion can be overcome by the quantum effect in which nuclei can tunnel through
      <a href="/wiki/Coulomb%27s_law" title="Coulomb's law">
       columb forces
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      When a
      <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">
       nucleon
      </a>
      such as a
      <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">
       proton
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">
       neutron
      </a>
      is added to a nucleus, the nuclear force attracts it to all the other nucleons of the nucleus (if the atom is small enough), but primarily to its immediate neighbours due to the short range of the force. The nucleons in the interior of a nucleus have more neighboring nucleons than those on the surface. Since smaller nuclei have a larger surface area-to-volume ratio, the binding energy per nucleon due to the
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_force" title="Nuclear force">
       nuclear force
      </a>
      generally increases with the size of the nucleus but approaches a limiting value corresponding to that of a nucleus with a diameter of about four nucleons. It is important to keep in mind that nucleons are
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_physics" title="Quantum physics">
       quantum objects
      </a>
      . So, for example, since two neutrons in a nucleus are identical to each other, the goal of distinguishing one from the other, such as which one is in the interior and which is on the surface, is in fact meaningless, and the inclusion of quantum mechanics is therefore necessary for proper calculations.
     </p>
     <p>
      The electrostatic force, on the other hand, is an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inverse_square_law" title="Inverse square law">
       inverse-square force
      </a>
      , so a proton added to a nucleus will feel an electrostatic repulsion from
      <i>
       all
      </i>
      the other protons in the nucleus. The electrostatic energy per nucleon due to the electrostatic force thus increases without limit as nuclei atomic number grows.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_fusion_forces_diagram.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="534" data-file-width="1186" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nuclear_fusion_forces_diagram.svg/350px-Nuclear_fusion_forces_diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nuclear_fusion_forces_diagram.svg/525px-Nuclear_fusion_forces_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nuclear_fusion_forces_diagram.svg/700px-Nuclear_fusion_forces_diagram.svg.png 2x" width="350"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_fusion_forces_diagram.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrostatic_force" title="Electrostatic force">
         electrostatic force
        </a>
        between the positively charged nuclei is repulsive, but when the separation is small enough, the quantum effect will tunnel through the wall. Therefore, the prerequisite for fusion is that the two nuclei be brought close enough together for a long enough time for quantum tunnelling to act.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The net result of the opposing electrostatic and strong nuclear forces is that the binding energy per nucleon generally increases with increasing size, up to the elements
      <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">
       iron
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">
       nickel
      </a>
      , and then decreases for heavier nuclei. Eventually, the
      <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">
       binding energy
      </a>
      becomes negative and very heavy nuclei (all with more than 208 nucleons, corresponding to a diameter of about 6 nucleons) are not stable. The four most tightly bound nuclei, in decreasing order of
      <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">
       binding energy
      </a>
      per nucleon, are
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a href="/wiki/Nickel-62" title="Nickel-62">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         62
         <br/>
        </span>
        Ni
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      ,
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron-58" title="Iron-58">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         58
         <br/>
        </span>
        Fe
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      ,
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a href="/wiki/Iron-56" title="Iron-56">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         56
         <br/>
        </span>
        Fe
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      , and
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nickel-60" title="Nickel-60">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         60
         <br/>
        </span>
        Ni
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Even though the
      <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_nickel" title="Isotopes of nickel">
       nickel isotope
      </a>
      ,
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        62
        <br/>
       </span>
       Ni
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      , is more stable, the
      <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_iron" title="Isotopes of iron">
       iron isotope
      </a>
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        56
        <br/>
       </span>
       Fe
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      is an
      <a href="/wiki/Order_of_magnitude" title="Order of magnitude">
       order of magnitude
      </a>
      more common. This is due to the fact that there is no easy way for stars to create
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        62
        <br/>
       </span>
       Ni
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      through the alpha process.
     </p>
     <p>
      An exception to this general trend is the
      <a href="/wiki/Helium-4" title="Helium-4">
       helium-4
      </a>
      nucleus, whose binding energy is higher than that of
      <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">
       lithium
      </a>
      , the next heaviest element. This is because protons and neutrons are
      <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">
       fermions
      </a>
      , which according to the
      <a href="/wiki/Pauli_exclusion_principle" title="Pauli exclusion principle">
       Pauli exclusion principle
      </a>
      cannot exist in the same nucleus in exactly the same state. Each proton or neutron energy state in a nucleus can accommodate both a spin up particle and a spin down particle. Helium-4 has an anomalously large binding energy because its nucleus consists of two protons and two neutrons, so all four of its nucleons can be in the ground state. Any additional nucleons would have to go into higher energy states. Indeed, the helium-4 nucleus is so tightly bound that it is commonly treated as a single particle in nuclear physics, namely, the
      <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">
       alpha particle
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The situation is similar if two nuclei are brought together. As they approach each other, all the protons in one nucleus repel all the protons in the other. Not until the two nuclei actually come close enough for long enough can the strong
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_force" title="Nuclear force">
       nuclear force
      </a>
      take over (by way of tunneling). Consequently, even when the final energy state is lower, there is a large energy barrier that must first be overcome. It is called the
      <a href="/wiki/Coulomb_barrier" title="Coulomb barrier">
       Coulomb barrier
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The Coulomb barrier is smallest for isotopes of hydrogen, as their nuclei contain only a single positive charge. A
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diproton" title="Diproton">
       diproton
      </a>
      is not stable, so neutrons must also be involved, ideally in such a way that a helium nucleus, with its extremely tight binding, is one of the products.
     </p>
     <p>
      Using
      <a href="/wiki/Tritium#Deuterium" title="Tritium">
       deuterium-tritium
      </a>
      fuel, the resulting energy barrier is about 0.1 MeV. In comparison, the energy needed to remove an
      <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">
       electron
      </a>
      from
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
       hydrogen
      </a>
      is 13.6 eV, about 7500 times less energy. The (intermediate) result of the fusion is an unstable
      <sup>
       5
      </sup>
      He nucleus, which immediately ejects a neutron with 14.1 MeV. The recoil energy of the remaining
      <sup>
       4
      </sup>
      He nucleus is 3.5 MeV, so the total energy liberated is 17.6 MeV. This is many times more than what was needed to overcome the energy barrier.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fusion_rxnrate.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="405" data-file-width="540" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Fusion_rxnrate.svg/300px-Fusion_rxnrate.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Fusion_rxnrate.svg/450px-Fusion_rxnrate.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Fusion_rxnrate.svg/600px-Fusion_rxnrate.svg.png 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fusion_rxnrate.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The fusion reaction rate increases rapidly with temperature until it maximizes and then gradually drops off. The DT rate peaks at a lower temperature (about 70 keV, or 800 million kelvin) and at a higher value than other reactions commonly considered for fusion energy.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The reaction
      <b>
       <a href="/wiki/Cross_section_(physics)" title="Cross section (physics)">
        cross section
       </a>
      </b>
      σ is a measure of the probability of a fusion reaction as a function of the relative velocity of the two reactant nuclei. If the reactants have a distribution of velocities, e.g. a thermal distribution, then it is useful to perform an average over the distributions of the product of cross section and velocity. This average is called the 'reactivity', denoted &lt;σv&gt;. The reaction rate (fusions per volume per time) is &lt;σv&gt; times the product of the reactant number densities:
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <span>
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              f
             </mi>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               n
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mn>
                1
               </mn>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               n
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mn>
                2
               </mn>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo fence="false" stretchy="false">
              ⟨
              <!-- ⟨ -->
             </mo>
             <mi>
              σ
              <!-- σ -->
             </mi>
             <mi>
              v
             </mi>
             <mo fence="false" stretchy="false">
              ⟩
              <!-- ⟩ -->
             </mo>
             <mo>
              .
             </mo>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle f=n_{1}n_{2}\langle \sigma v\rangle .}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="f=n_{1}n_{2}\langle \sigma v\rangle ." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0b9a8d14268990bd84ad6e3d04a8fd2f2f044370" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:14.297ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
       </span>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      If a species of nuclei is reacting with itself, such as the DD reaction, then the product
      <span>
       <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
        <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
         <semantics>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
           <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
            <msub>
             <mi>
              n
             </mi>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mn>
               1
              </mn>
             </mrow>
            </msub>
            <msub>
             <mi>
              n
             </mi>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mn>
               2
              </mn>
             </mrow>
            </msub>
           </mstyle>
          </mrow>
          <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
           {\displaystyle n_{1}n_{2}}
          </annotation>
         </semantics>
        </math>
       </span>
       <img alt="n_{1}n_{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a70ec72e416c0e2120ab2480be2bf67cc40817bd" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:4.934ex; height:2.009ex;"/>
      </span>
      must be replaced by
      <span>
       <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
        <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
         <semantics>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
           <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
            <mo stretchy="false">
             (
            </mo>
            <mn>
             1
            </mn>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mo>
              /
             </mo>
            </mrow>
            <mn>
             2
            </mn>
            <mo stretchy="false">
             )
            </mo>
            <msup>
             <mi>
              n
             </mi>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mn>
               2
              </mn>
             </mrow>
            </msup>
           </mstyle>
          </mrow>
          <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
           {\displaystyle (1/2)n^{2}}
          </annotation>
         </semantics>
        </math>
       </span>
       <img alt="(1/2)n^{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e85e75cfa5d70ab77768fedfb8c7957cc6fd3808" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:7.816ex; height:3.176ex;"/>
      </span>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      <span>
       <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
        <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
         <semantics>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
           <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
            <mo fence="false" stretchy="false">
             ⟨
             <!-- ⟨ -->
            </mo>
            <mi>
             σ
             <!-- σ -->
            </mi>
            <mi>
             v
            </mi>
            <mo fence="false" stretchy="false">
             ⟩
             <!-- ⟩ -->
            </mo>
           </mstyle>
          </mrow>
          <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
           {\displaystyle \langle \sigma v\rangle }
          </annotation>
         </semantics>
        </math>
       </span>
       <img alt="\langle \sigma v\rangle " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c32624c6fcf974ef4aa9bfc7a9e691757fbd523f" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.308ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
      </span>
      increases from virtually zero at room temperatures up to meaningful magnitudes at temperatures of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1_E-15_J" title="1 E-15 J">
       10
      </a>
      –
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1_E-14_J" title="1 E-14 J">
       100
      </a>
      keV. At these temperatures, well above typical
      <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">
       ionization
      </a>
      energies (13.6 eV in the hydrogen case), the fusion reactants exist in a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_physics" title="Plasma physics">
       plasma
      </a>
      state.
     </p>
     <p>
      The significance of
      <span>
       <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
        <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
         <semantics>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
           <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
            <mo fence="false" stretchy="false">
             ⟨
             <!-- ⟨ -->
            </mo>
            <mi>
             σ
             <!-- σ -->
            </mi>
            <mi>
             v
            </mi>
            <mo fence="false" stretchy="false">
             ⟩
             <!-- ⟩ -->
            </mo>
           </mstyle>
          </mrow>
          <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
           {\displaystyle \langle \sigma v\rangle }
          </annotation>
         </semantics>
        </math>
       </span>
       <img alt="\langle \sigma v\rangle " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c32624c6fcf974ef4aa9bfc7a9e691757fbd523f" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.308ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
      </span>
      as a function of temperature in a device with a particular energy
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confinement_time" title="Confinement time">
       confinement time
      </a>
      is found by considering the
      <a href="/wiki/Lawson_criterion" title="Lawson criterion">
       Lawson criterion
      </a>
      . This is an extremely challenging barrier to overcome on Earth, which explains why fusion research has taken many years to reach the current high state of technical prowess.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lawson_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lawson-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Methods_for_achieving_fusion">
       Methods for achieving fusion
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Methods for achieving fusion">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Fusion_power" title="Fusion power">
       Fusion power
      </a>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Thermonuclear_fusion">
       Thermonuclear fusion
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Thermonuclear fusion">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Thermonuclear_fusion" title="Thermonuclear fusion">
       Thermonuclear fusion
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      If the matter is sufficiently heated (hence being
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_physics" title="Plasma physics">
       plasma
      </a>
      ), the fusion reaction may occur due to collisions with extreme thermal kinetic energies of the particles. In the form of thermonuclear weapons, thermonuclear fusion is the only fusion technique so far to yield undeniably large amounts of useful
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fusion_energy" title="Fusion energy">
       fusion energy
      </a>
      . Usable amounts of thermonuclear fusion energy released in a controlled manner have yet to be achieved. In nature, this is what produces energy in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stars" title="Stars">
       stars
      </a>
      through
      <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">
       stellar nucleosynthesis
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Inertial_confinement_fusion">
       Inertial confinement fusion
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Inertial confinement fusion">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Inertial_confinement_fusion" title="Inertial confinement fusion">
       Inertial confinement fusion
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Inertial_confinement_fusion" title="Inertial confinement fusion">
       Inertial confinement fusion
      </a>
      (
      <b>
       ICF
      </b>
      ) is a type of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fusion_energy" title="Fusion energy">
       fusion energy
      </a>
      research that attempts to initiate nuclear fusion reactions by heating and compressing a fuel target, typically in the form of a pellet that most often contains a mixture of
      <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">
       deuterium
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">
       tritium
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Inertial_electrostatic_confinement">
       Inertial electrostatic confinement
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Inertial electrostatic confinement">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Inertial_electrostatic_confinement" title="Inertial electrostatic confinement">
       Inertial electrostatic confinement
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Inertial_electrostatic_confinement" title="Inertial electrostatic confinement">
       Inertial electrostatic confinement
      </a>
      is a set of devices that use an electric field to heat ions to fusion conditions. The most well known is the
      <a href="/wiki/Fusor" title="Fusor">
       fusor
      </a>
      . Starting in 1999, a number of amateurs have been able to do amateur fusion using these homemade devices.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        [11]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        [15]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other IEC devices include: the
      <a href="/wiki/Polywell" title="Polywell">
       Polywell
      </a>
      , MIX POPS
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and Marble concepts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        [17]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Beam-beam_or_beam-target_fusion">
       Beam-beam or beam-target fusion
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Beam-beam or beam-target fusion">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      If the energy to initiate the reaction comes from
      <a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">
       accelerating
      </a>
      one of the nuclei, the process is called
      <i>
       beam-target
      </i>
      fusion; if both nuclei are accelerated, it is
      <i>
       beam-beam
      </i>
      fusion.
     </p>
     <p>
      Accelerator-based light-ion fusion is a technique using particle accelerators to achieve particle kinetic energies sufficient to induce light-ion fusion reactions. Accelerating light ions is relatively easy, and can be done in an efficient manner—requiring only a vacuum tube, a pair of electrodes, and a high-voltage transformer; fusion can be observed with as little as 10 kV between the electrodes. The key problem with accelerator-based fusion (and with cold targets in general) is that fusion cross sections are many orders of magnitude lower than Coulomb interaction cross sections. Therefore, the vast majority of ions expend their energy emitting
      <a href="/wiki/Bremsstrahlung" title="Bremsstrahlung">
       bremsstrahlung
      </a>
      radiation and the ionization of atoms of the target. Devices referred to as sealed-tube
      <a href="/wiki/Neutron_generator" title="Neutron generator">
       neutron generators
      </a>
      are particularly relevant to this discussion. These small devices are miniature particle accelerators filled with deuterium and tritium gas in an arrangement that allows ions of those nuclei to be accelerated against hydride targets, also containing deuterium and tritium, where fusion takes place, releasing a flux of neutrons. Hundreds of neutron generators are produced annually for use in the petroleum industry where they are used in measurement equipment for locating and mapping oil reserves.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Muon-catalyzed_fusion">
       Muon-catalyzed fusion
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Muon-catalyzed fusion">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Muon-catalyzed_fusion" title="Muon-catalyzed fusion">
       Muon-catalyzed fusion
      </a>
      is a well-established and reproducible fusion process that occurs at ordinary temperatures. It was studied in detail by
      <a href="/wiki/Steven_E._Jones" title="Steven E. Jones">
       Steven Jones
      </a>
      in the early 1980s. Net energy production from this reaction cannot occur because of the high energy required to create
      <a href="/wiki/Muon" title="Muon">
       muons
      </a>
      , their short 2.2 µs
      <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">
       half-life
      </a>
      , and the high chance that a muon will bind to the new
      <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">
       alpha particle
      </a>
      and thus stop catalyzing fusion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        [18]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_principles">
       Other principles
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Other principles">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TCV_vue_gen.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="3072" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/TCV_vue_gen.jpg/220px-TCV_vue_gen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/TCV_vue_gen.jpg/330px-TCV_vue_gen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/TCV_vue_gen.jpg/440px-TCV_vue_gen.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TCV_vue_gen.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Tokamak_%C3%A0_configuration_variable" title="Tokamak à configuration variable">
          Tokamak à configuration variable
         </a>
        </i>
        , research fusion reactor, at the
        <a href="/wiki/%C3%89cole_Polytechnique_F%C3%A9d%C3%A9rale_de_Lausanne" title="École Polytechnique Fédérale de Lausanne">
         École Polytechnique Fédérale de Lausanne
        </a>
        (Switzerland).
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Some other confinement principles have been investigated.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antimatter_catalyzed_nuclear_pulse_propulsion" title="Antimatter catalyzed nuclear pulse propulsion">
       Antimatter-initialized fusion
      </a>
      uses small amounts of
      <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">
       antimatter
      </a>
      to trigger a tiny fusion explosion. This has been studied primarily in the context of making
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_pulse_propulsion" title="Nuclear pulse propulsion">
       nuclear pulse propulsion
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pure_fusion_bomb" title="Pure fusion bomb">
       pure fusion bombs
      </a>
      feasible. This is not near becoming a practical power source, due to the cost of manufacturing antimatter alone.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Pyroelectric_fusion" title="Pyroelectric fusion">
       Pyroelectric fusion
      </a>
      was reported in April 2005 by a team at
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Los_Angeles" title="University of California, Los Angeles">
       UCLA
      </a>
      . The scientists used a
      <a href="/wiki/Pyroelectricity" title="Pyroelectricity">
       pyroelectric
      </a>
      crystal heated from −34 to 7 °C (−29 to 45 °F), combined with a
      <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">
       tungsten
      </a>
      needle to produce an
      <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">
       electric field
      </a>
      of about 25 gigavolts per meter to ionize and accelerate
      <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">
       deuterium
      </a>
      nuclei into an
      <a href="/wiki/Erbium" title="Erbium">
       erbium
      </a>
      deuteride target. At the estimated energy levels,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
      the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion_reactions" title="Nuclear fusion reactions">
       D-D fusion reaction
      </a>
      may occur, producing
      <a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3">
       helium-3
      </a>
      and a 2.45 MeV
      <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">
       neutron
      </a>
      . Although it makes a useful neutron generator, the apparatus is not intended for power generation since it requires far more energy than it produces.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        [20]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion-fission_hybrid" title="Nuclear fusion-fission hybrid">
       Hybrid nuclear fusion-fission (hybrid nuclear power)
      </a>
      is a proposed means of generating
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_power_industry" title="Electrical power industry">
       power
      </a>
      by use of a combination of nuclear fusion and
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">
       fission
      </a>
      processes. The concept dates to the 1950s, and was briefly advocated by
      <a href="/wiki/Hans_Bethe" title="Hans Bethe">
       Hans Bethe
      </a>
      during the 1970s, but largely remained unexplored until a revival of interest in 2009, due to the delays in the realization of pure fusion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hybrid_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hybrid-24">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Project_PACER" title="Project PACER">
       Project PACER
      </a>
      , carried out at
      <a href="/wiki/Los_Alamos_National_Laboratory" title="Los Alamos National Laboratory">
       Los Alamos National Laboratory
      </a>
      (LANL) in the mid-1970s, explored the possibility of a fusion power system that would involve exploding small
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H-bomb" title="H-bomb">
       hydrogen bombs
      </a>
      (fusion bombs) inside an underground cavity. As an energy source, the system is the only fusion power system that could be demonstrated to work using existing technology. However it would also require a large, continuous supply of nuclear bombs, making the economics of such a system rather questionable.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Important_reactions">
       Important reactions
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Important reactions">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Astrophysical_reaction_chains">
       Astrophysical reaction chains
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Astrophysical reaction chains">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      At the temperatures and densities in stellar cores the rates of fusion reactions are notoriously slow. For example, at solar core temperature (
      <i>
       T
      </i>
      ≈ 15 MK) and density (160 g/cm
      <sup>
       3
      </sup>
      ), the energy release rate is only 276 μW/cm
      <sup>
       3
      </sup>
      —about a quarter of the volumetric rate at which a resting human body generates heat.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thus, reproduction of stellar core conditions in a lab for nuclear fusion power production is completely impractical. Because nuclear reaction rates depend on density as well as temperature and most fusion scemes operate at relatively low densities, those methods are strongly dependent on higher temperatures. The fusion rate as a function of temperature (exp(−
      <i>
       E
      </i>
      /
      <i>
       kT
      </i>
      )), leads to the need to achieve temperatures in terrestrial reactors 10–100 times higher temperatures and in stellar interiors:
      <i>
       T
      </i>
      ≈ 0.1–1.0×10
      <sup>
       9
      </sup>
      K.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Criteria_and_candidates_for_terrestrial_reactions">
       Criteria and candidates for terrestrial reactions
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Criteria and candidates for terrestrial reactions">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Fusion_power#Fuels" title="Fusion power">
       Fusion power § Fuels
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      In artificial fusion, the primary fuel is not constrained to be protons and higher temperatures can be used, so reactions with larger cross-sections are chosen. Another concern is the production of neutrons, which activate the reactor structure radiologically, but also have the advantages of allowing volumetric extraction of the fusion energy and
      <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">
       tritium
      </a>
      breeding. Reactions that release no neutrons are referred to as
      <a href="/wiki/Aneutronic_fusion" title="Aneutronic fusion">
       <i>
        aneutronic
       </i>
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      To be a useful energy source, a fusion reaction must satisfy several criteria. It must:
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       Be
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exothermic" title="Exothermic">
        exothermic
       </a>
      </dt>
      <dd>
       This limits the reactants to the low
       <i>
        Z
       </i>
       (number of protons) side of the
       <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_binding_energy#Nuclear_binding_energy_curve" title="Nuclear binding energy">
        curve of binding energy
       </a>
       . It also makes helium
       <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
        <a href="/wiki/Helium-4" title="Helium-4">
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
          4
          <br/>
         </span>
         He
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
          <br/>
         </span>
        </a>
       </span>
       the most common product because of its extraordinarily tight binding, although
       <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
        <a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3">
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
          3
          <br/>
         </span>
         He
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
          <br/>
         </span>
        </a>
       </span>
       and
       <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen-3" title="Hydrogen-3">
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
          3
          <br/>
         </span>
         H
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
          <br/>
         </span>
        </a>
       </span>
       also show up.
      </dd>
      <dt>
       Involve low atomic number (
       <i>
        Z
       </i>
       ) nuclei
      </dt>
      <dd>
       This is because the electrostatic repulsion must be overcome before the nuclei are close enough to fuse.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This implies a relationship between atomic number (Z) and activation energy (in addition to its relation to binding energy), which isn't established/cited here. (November 2015)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </dd>
      <dt>
       Have two reactants
      </dt>
      <dd>
       At anything less than stellar densities, three body collisions are too improbable. In inertial confinement, both stellar densities and temperatures are exceeded to compensate for the shortcomings of the third parameter of the Lawson criterion, ICF's very short confinement time.
      </dd>
      <dt>
       Have two or more products
      </dt>
      <dd>
       This allows simultaneous conservation of energy and momentum without relying on the electromagnetic force.
      </dd>
      <dt>
       Conserve both protons and neutrons
      </dt>
      <dd>
       The cross sections for the weak interaction are too small.
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Few reactions meet these criteria. The following are those with the largest cross sections:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
