     <p>
      For reactions with two products, the energy is divided between them in inverse proportion to their masses, as shown. In most reactions with three products, the distribution of energy varies. For reactions that can result in more than one set of products, the branching ratios are given.
     </p>
     <p>
      Some reaction candidates can be eliminated at once. The D-
      <sup>
       6
      </sup>
      Li reaction has no advantage compared to
      <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">
       p
       <sup>
        +
       </sup>
      </a>
      -
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boron-11" title="Boron-11">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         11
         <br/>
         5
        </span>
        B
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      because it is roughly as difficult to burn but produces substantially more neutrons through
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         2
         <br/>
         1
        </span>
        D
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      -
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         2
         <br/>
         1
        </span>
        D
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      side reactions. There is also a
      <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">
       p
       <sup>
        +
       </sup>
      </a>
      -
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lithium-7" title="Lithium-7">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         7
         <br/>
         3
        </span>
        Li
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      reaction, but the cross section is far too low, except possibly when
      <i>
       T
      </i>
      <sub>
       i
      </sub>
      &gt; 1 MeV, but at such high temperatures an endothermic, direct neutron-producing reaction also becomes very significant. Finally there is also a
      <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">
       p
       <sup>
        +
       </sup>
      </a>
      -
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beryllium-9" title="Beryllium-9">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         9
         <br/>
         4
        </span>
        Be
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      reaction, which is not only difficult to burn, but
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beryllium-9" title="Beryllium-9">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         9
         <br/>
         4
        </span>
        Be
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      can be easily induced to split into two alpha particles and a neutron.
     </p>
     <p>
      In addition to the fusion reactions, the following reactions with neutrons are important in order to "breed" tritium in "dry" fusion bombs and some proposed fusion reactors:
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <table border="0">
        <tr style="height:2em;">
         <td>
          <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">
           n
           <sup>
            0
           </sup>
          </a>
         </td>
         <td>
          +
         </td>
         <td>
          <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lithium-6" title="Lithium-6">
            <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
             6
             <br/>
             3
            </span>
            Li
            <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
             <br/>
            </span>
           </a>
          </span>
         </td>
         <td>
          →
         </td>
         <td>
          <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
           <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">
            <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
             3
             <br/>
             1
            </span>
            T
            <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
             <br/>
            </span>
           </a>
          </span>
         </td>
         <td>
          +
         </td>
         <td>
          <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
           <a href="/wiki/Helium-4" title="Helium-4">
            <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
             4
             <br/>
             2
            </span>
            He
            <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
             <br/>
            </span>
           </a>
          </span>
          + 4.784 MeV
         </td>
        </tr>
        <tr style="height:2em;">
         <td>
          <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">
           n
           <sup>
            0
           </sup>
          </a>
         </td>
         <td>
          +
         </td>
         <td>
          <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lithium-7" title="Lithium-7">
            <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
             7
             <br/>
             3
            </span>
            Li
            <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
             <br/>
            </span>
           </a>
          </span>
         </td>
         <td>
          →
         </td>
         <td>
          <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
           <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">
            <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
             3
             <br/>
             1
            </span>
            T
            <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
             <br/>
            </span>
           </a>
          </span>
         </td>
         <td>
          +
         </td>
         <td>
          <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
           <a href="/wiki/Helium-4" title="Helium-4">
            <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
             4
             <br/>
             2
            </span>
            He
            <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
             <br/>
            </span>
           </a>
          </span>
          +
          <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">
           n
           <sup>
            0
           </sup>
          </a>
          – 2.467 MeV
         </td>
        </tr>
       </table>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      The latter of the two equations was unknown when the U.S. conducted the
      <a href="/wiki/Castle_Bravo" title="Castle Bravo">
       Castle Bravo
      </a>
      fusion bomb test in 1954. Being just the second fusion bomb ever tested (and the first to use lithium), the designers of the Castle Bravo "Shrimp" had understood the usefulness of Lithium-6 in tritium production, but had failed to recognize that Lithium-7 fission would greatly increase the yield of the bomb. While Li-7 has a small neutron cross-section for low neutron energies, it has a higher cross section above 5 MeV.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cross_section_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cross_section-27">
        [27]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Li-7 also undergoes a chain reaction due to its release of a neutron after fissioning. The 15 Mt yield was 150% greater than the predicted 6 Mt and caused casualties from the fallout generated.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      To evaluate the usefulness of these reactions, in addition to the reactants, the products, and the energy released, one needs to know something about the cross section. Any given fusion device has a maximum plasma pressure it can sustain, and an economical device would always operate near this maximum. Given this pressure, the largest fusion output is obtained when the temperature is chosen so that &lt;σv&gt;/T
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      is a maximum. This is also the temperature at which the value of the triple product
      <i>
       nT
      </i>
      τ required for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fusion_gain_factor" title="Fusion gain factor">
       ignition
      </a>
      is a minimum, since that required value is inversely proportional to &lt;σv&gt;/T
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      (see
      <a href="/wiki/Lawson_criterion" title="Lawson criterion">
       Lawson criterion
      </a>
      ). (A plasma is "ignited" if the fusion reactions produce enough power to maintain the temperature without external heating.) This optimum temperature and the value of &lt;σv&gt;/T
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      at that temperature is given for a few of these reactions in the following table.
     </p>

     <p>
      Note that many of the reactions form chains. For instance, a reactor fueled with
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        3
        <br/>
        1
       </span>
       T
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      and
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        3
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       He
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      creates some
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        2
        <br/>
        1
       </span>
       D
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      , which is then possible to use in the
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        2
        <br/>
        1
       </span>
       D
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      -
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        3
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       He
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      reaction if the energies are "right". An elegant idea is to combine the reactions (8) and (9). The
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        3
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       He
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      from reaction (8) can react with
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        6
        <br/>
        3
       </span>
       Li
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      in reaction (9) before completely thermalizing. This produces an energetic proton, which in turn undergoes reaction (8) before thermalizing. Detailed analysis shows that this idea would not work well,
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2010)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      but it is a good example of a case where the usual assumption of a
      <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%E2%80%93Boltzmann_distribution" title="Maxwell–Boltzmann distribution">
       Maxwellian
      </a>
      plasma is not appropriate.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Neutronicity.2C_confinement_requirement.2C_and_power_density">
       Neutronicity, confinement requirement, and power density
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Neutronicity, confinement requirement, and power density">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IvyMike2.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1098" data-file-width="1470" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/IvyMike2.jpg/350px-IvyMike2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/IvyMike2.jpg/525px-IvyMike2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/IvyMike2.jpg/700px-IvyMike2.jpg 2x" width="350"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IvyMike2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The only man-made fusion device to achieve
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fusion_gain_factor" title="Fusion gain factor">
         ignition
        </a>
        to date is the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bomb" title="Hydrogen bomb">
         hydrogen bomb
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
         [
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
           <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
            citation needed
           </span>
          </a>
         </i>
         ]
        </sup>
        The detonation of the first device, codenamed
        <a href="/wiki/Ivy_Mike" title="Ivy Mike">
         Ivy Mike
        </a>
        , occurred in 1952 and is shown here.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Any of the reactions above can in principle be the basis of
      <a href="/wiki/Fusion_power" title="Fusion power">
       fusion power
      </a>
      production. In addition to the temperature and cross section discussed above, we must consider the total energy of the fusion products
      <i>
       E
      </i>
      <sub>
       fus
      </sub>
      , the energy of the charged fusion products
      <i>
       E
      </i>
      <sub>
       ch
      </sub>
      , and the atomic number
      <i>
       Z
      </i>
      of the non-hydrogenic reactant.
     </p>
     <p>
      Specification of the
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        2
        <br/>
        1
       </span>
       D
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      -
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        2
        <br/>
        1
       </span>
       D
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      reaction entails some difficulties, though. To begin with, one must average over the two branches (2i) and (2ii). More difficult is to decide how to treat the
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        3
        <br/>
        1
       </span>
       T
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      and
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        3
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       He
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      products.
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        3
        <br/>
        1
       </span>
       T
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      burns so well in a deuterium plasma that it is almost impossible to extract from the plasma. The
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        2
        <br/>
        1
       </span>
       D
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      -
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        3
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       He
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      reaction is optimized at a much higher temperature, so the burnup at the optimum
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        2
        <br/>
        1
       </span>
       D
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      -
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        2
        <br/>
        1
       </span>
       D
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      temperature may be low, so it seems reasonable to assume the
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        3
        <br/>
        1
       </span>
       T
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      but not the
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        3
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       He
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      gets burned up and adds its energy to the net reaction. Thus the total reaction would be the sum of (2i), (2ii), and (1):
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       5
       <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
        <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
          2
          <br/>
          1
         </span>
         D
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
          <br/>
         </span>
        </a>
       </span>
       →
       <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
        <a href="/wiki/Helium-4" title="Helium-4">
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
          4
          <br/>
          2
         </span>
         He
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
          <br/>
         </span>
        </a>
       </span>
       + 2
       <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">
        n
        <sup>
         0
        </sup>
       </a>
       +
       <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
        <a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3">
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
          3
          <br/>
          2
         </span>
         He
         <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
          <br/>
         </span>
        </a>
       </span>
       +
       <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">
        p
        <sup>
         +
        </sup>
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        E
       </i>
       <sub>
        fus
       </sub>
       = 4.03+17.6+3.27 = 24.9 MeV,
       <i>
        E
       </i>
       <sub>
        ch
       </sub>
       = 4.03+3.5+0.82 = 8.35 MeV.
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      We count the
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        2
        <br/>
        1
       </span>
       D
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      -
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        2
        <br/>
        1
       </span>
       D
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      fusion energy
      <i>
       per D-D reaction
      </i>
      (not per pair of deuterium atoms) as
      <i>
       E
      </i>
      <sub>
       fus
      </sub>
      = (4.03 MeV + 17.6 MeV)×50% + (3.27 MeV)×50% = 12.5 MeV and the energy in charged particles as
      <i>
       E
      </i>
      <sub>
       ch
      </sub>
      = (4.03 MeV + 3.5 MeV)×50% + (0.82 MeV)×50% = 4.2 MeV. (Note: if the tritium ion reacts with a deuteron while it still has a large kinetic energy, then the kinetic energy of the helium-4 produced may be quite different from 3.5 MeV, so this calculation of energy in charged particles is only approximate.)
     </p>
     <p>
      Another unique aspect of the
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        2
        <br/>
        1
       </span>
       D
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      -
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        2
        <br/>
        1
       </span>
       D
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      reaction is that there is only one reactant, which must be taken into account when calculating the reaction rate.
     </p>
     <p>
      With this choice, we tabulate parameters for four of the most important reactions

     <p>
      The last column is the
      <b>
       <a href="/wiki/Aneutronic_fusion" title="Aneutronic fusion">
        neutronicity
       </a>
      </b>
      of the reaction, the fraction of the fusion energy released as neutrons. This is an important indicator of the magnitude of the problems associated with neutrons like radiation damage, biological shielding, remote handling, and safety. For the first two reactions it is calculated as (
      <i>
       E
      </i>
      <sub>
       fus
      </sub>
      -
      <i>
       E
      </i>
      <sub>
       ch
      </sub>
      )/
      <i>
       E
      </i>
      <sub>
       fus
      </sub>
      . For the last two reactions, where this calculation would give zero, the values quoted are rough estimates based on side reactions that produce neutrons in a plasma in thermal equilibrium.
     </p>
     <p>
      Of course, the reactants should also be mixed in the optimal proportions. This is the case when each reactant ion plus its associated electrons accounts for half the pressure. Assuming that the total pressure is fixed, this means that density of the non-hydrogenic ion is smaller than that of the hydrogenic ion by a factor 2/(
      <i>
       Z
      </i>
      +1). Therefore, the rate for these reactions is reduced by the same factor, on top of any differences in the values of &lt;σv&gt;/T
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      . On the other hand, because the
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        2
        <br/>
        1
       </span>
       D
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      -
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        2
        <br/>
        1
       </span>
       D
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      reaction has only one reactant, its rate is twice as high as when the fuel is divided between two different hydrogenic species, thus creating a more efficient reaction.
     </p>
     <p>
      Thus there is a "penalty" of (2/(Z+1)) for non-hydrogenic fuels arising from the fact that they require more electrons, which take up pressure without participating in the fusion reaction. (It is usually a good assumption that the electron temperature will be nearly equal to the ion temperature. Some authors, however discuss the possibility that the electrons could be maintained substantially colder than the ions. In such a case, known as a "hot ion mode", the "penalty" would not apply.) There is at the same time a "bonus" of a factor 2 for
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        2
        <br/>
        1
       </span>
       D
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      -
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
        2
        <br/>
        1
       </span>
       D
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
       </span>
      </span>
      because each ion can react with any of the other ions, not just a fraction of them.
     </p>

      </span>

      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">

         <br/>

        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      reaction under comparable conditions. The column "
      <a href="/wiki/Lawson_criterion" title="Lawson criterion">
       Lawson criterion
      </a>
      " weights these results with
      <i>
       E
      </i>
      <sub>
       ch
      </sub>
      and gives an indication of how much more difficult it is to achieve ignition with these reactions, relative to the difficulty for the
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         2
         <br/>
         1
        </span>
        D
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      -
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         3
         <br/>
         1
        </span>

        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      reaction. The last column is labeled "power density" and weights the practical reactivity with
      <i>
       E
      </i>
      <sub>
       fus
      </sub>
      . It indicates how much lower the fusion power density of the other reactions is compared to the
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         2
         <br/>
         1
        </span>
        D
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      -
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         3
         <br/>
         1
        </span>
        T
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      reaction and can be considered a measure of the economic potential.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Bremsstrahlung_losses_in_quasineutral.2C_isotropic_plasmas">
       Bremsstrahlung losses in quasineutral, isotropic plasmas
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Bremsstrahlung losses in quasineutral, isotropic plasmas">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The ions undergoing fusion in many systems will essentially never occur alone but will be mixed with
      <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">
       electrons
      </a>
      that in aggregate neutralize the ions' bulk
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_charge" title="Electrical charge">
       electrical charge
      </a>
      and form a
      <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">
       plasma
      </a>
      . The electrons will generally have a temperature comparable to or greater than that of the ions, so they will collide with the ions and emit
      <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">
       x-ray
      </a>
      radiation of 10–30 keV energy, a process known as
      <a href="/wiki/Bremsstrahlung" title="Bremsstrahlung">
       Bremsstrahlung
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The huge size of the Sun and stars means that the x-rays produced in this process will not escape and will deposit their energy back into the plasma. They are said to be
      <a href="/wiki/Opacity_(optics)" title="Opacity (optics)">
       opaque
      </a>
      to x-rays. But any terrestrial fusion reactor will be
      <a href="/wiki/Optical_depth" title="Optical depth">
       optically thin
      </a>
      for x-rays of this energy range. X-rays are difficult to reflect but they are effectively absorbed (and converted into heat) in less than mm thickness of stainless steel (which is part of a reactor's shield). This means the bremsstrahlung process is carrying energy out of the plasma, cooling it.
     </p>
     <p>
      The ratio of fusion power produced to x-ray radiation lost to walls is an important figure of merit. This ratio is generally maximized at a much higher temperature than that which maximizes the power density (see the previous subsection). The following table shows estimates of the optimum temperature and the power ratio at that temperature for several reactions.
     </p>
     <table class="wikitable" style="margin:auto;">
      <tr>
       <th>
        fuel

       </th>
       <th>
        <i>
         P
        </i>
        <sub>
         fusion
        </sub>
        /
        <i>
         P
        </i>
        <sub>
         Bremsstrahlung
        </sub>
       </th>
      </tr>

     <p>
      The actual ratios of fusion to Bremsstrahlung power will likely be significantly lower for several reasons. For one, the calculation assumes that the energy of the fusion products is transmitted completely to the fuel ions, which then lose energy to the electrons by collisions, which in turn lose energy by Bremsstrahlung. However, because the fusion products move much faster than the fuel ions, they will give up a significant fraction of their energy directly to the electrons. Secondly, the ions in the plasma are assumed to be purely fuel ions. In practice, there will be a significant proportion of impurity ions, which will then lower the ratio. In particular, the fusion products themselves
      <i>
       must
      </i>
      remain in the plasma until they have given up their energy, and
      <i>
       will
      </i>
      remain some time after that in any proposed confinement scheme. Finally, all channels of energy loss other than Bremsstrahlung have been neglected. The last two factors are related. On theoretical and experimental grounds, particle and energy confinement seem to be closely related. In a confinement scheme that does a good job of retaining energy, fusion products will build up. If the fusion products are efficiently ejected, then energy confinement will be poor, too.
     </p>
     <p>
      The temperatures maximizing the fusion power compared to the Bremsstrahlung are in every case higher than the temperature that maximizes the power density and minimizes the required value of the
      <a href="/wiki/Lawson_criterion" title="Lawson criterion">
       fusion triple product
      </a>
      . This will not change the optimum operating point for
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         2
         <br/>
         1
        </span>
        D
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      -
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         3
         <br/>
         1
        </span>
        T
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      very much because the Bremsstrahlung fraction is low, but it will push the other fuels into regimes where the power density relative to
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         2
         <br/>
         1
        </span>
        D
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      -
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         3
         <br/>
         1
        </span>
        T
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      is even lower and the required confinement even more difficult to achieve. For
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         2
         <br/>
         1
        </span>
        D
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      -
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         2
         <br/>
         1
        </span>
        D
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      and
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         2
         <br/>
         1
        </span>
        D
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      -
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         3
         <br/>
         2
        </span>
        He
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      , Bremsstrahlung losses will be a serious, possibly prohibitive problem. For
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         3
         <br/>
         2
        </span>
        He
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      -
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         3
         <br/>
         2
        </span>
        He
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">
       p
       <sup>
        +
       </sup>
      </a>
      -
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lithium-6" title="Lithium-6">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         6
         <br/>
         3
        </span>
        Li
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">
       p
       <sup>
        +
       </sup>
      </a>
      -
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boron-11" title="Boron-11">
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right">
         11
         <br/>
         5
        </span>
        B
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
        </span>
       </a>
      </span>
      the Bremsstrahlung losses appear to make a fusion reactor using these fuels with a quasineutral, isotropic plasma impossible. Some ways out of this dilemma are considered—and rejected—in
      <i>
       fundamental limitations on plasma fusion systems not in thermodynamic equilibrium
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This limitation does not apply to
      <a href="/wiki/Non-neutral_plasmas" title="Non-neutral plasmas">
       non-neutral and anisotropic plasmas
      </a>
      ; however, these have their own challenges to contend with.
     </p>

        M. Kikuchi, K. Lackner &amp; M. Q. Tran (2012).
        <a class="external text" href="http://www-pub.iaea.org/books/IAEABooks/8879/Fusion-Physics" rel="nofollow">
         <i>
          Fusion Physics
         </i>
        </a>
        .
        <a href="/wiki/International_Atomic_Energy_Agency" title="International Atomic Energy Agency">
         International Atomic Energy Agency
        </a>
        . p. 22.
        <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
         ISBN
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9789201304100" title="Special:BookSources/9789201304100">
         9789201304100
        </a>
        .
       </cite>
       <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANuclear+fusion&amp;rft.au=M.+Kikuchi%2C+K.+Lackner&amp;rft.au=M.+Q.+Tran&amp;rft.btitle=Fusion+Physics&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww-pub.iaea.org%2Fbooks%2FIAEABooks%2F8879%2FFusion-Physics&amp;rft.isbn=9789201304100&amp;rft.pages=22&amp;rft.pub=International+Atomic+Energy+Agency&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
